  <a href="#other_reviews" class="actionLinkLite votes">
      <span itemprop="ratingCount" title="11235" class="value-title">11,235 Ratings</span>
</a>  <span class="greyText"> · </span>

<div id="descriptionContainer">
      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer9302774933671716057"><strong>Crow Lake</strong> is that rare find, a first novel so quietly assured, so emotionally pitch perfect, you know from the opening page that this is the real thing—a literary experience in which to lose yourself, by an author of immense talent.<br /><br />Here is a gorgeous, slow-burning story set in the rural “badlands” of northern Ontario, where heartbreak and hardship are mirrored in the lands</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9302774933671716057"><strong>Crow Lake</strong> is that rare find, a first novel so quietly assured, so emotionally pitch perfect, you know from the opening page that this is the real thing—a literary experience in which to lose yourself, by an author of immense talent.<br /><br />Here is a gorgeous, slow-burning story set in the rural “badlands” of northern Ontario, where heartbreak and hardship are mirrored in the landscape. For the farming Pye family, life is a Greek tragedy where the sins of the fathers are visited on the sons, and terrible events occur—offstage. <br /><br />Centerstage are the Morrisons, whose tragedy looks more immediate if less brutal, but is, in reality, insidious and divisive. Orphaned young, Kate Morrison was her older brother Matt’s protegee, her fascination for pond life fed by his passionate interest in the natural world. Now a zoologist, she can identify organisms under a microscope but seems blind to the state of her own emotional life. And she thinks she’s outgrown her siblings—Luke, Matt, and Bo—who were once her entire world. <br /><br />In this universal drama of family love and misunderstandings, of resentments harbored and driven underground, Lawson ratchets up the tension with heartbreaking humor and consummate control, continually overturning one’s expectations right to the very end. Tragic, funny, unforgettable, <strong>Crow Lake</strong> is a quiet tour de force that will catapult Mary Lawson to the forefront of fiction writers today.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9302774933671716057">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1322390521">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1322390521" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/12656534-zoeytron" class="left imgcol" title="Zoeytron"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1451089497p2/12656534.jpg" alt="Zoeytron" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1322390521?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 02, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/12656534-zoeytron" class="user" title="Zoeytron">Zoeytron</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/12656534-zoeytron?shelf=public-library" class="actionLinkLite">public-library</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1322390521">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11584281845065376755"><em>'. . . just one more dropped stitch in a family tapestry full of holes.'</em><br /><br />This is a story of strong familial bonds forged early in life, then eroding from deep-seated resentments, guilt, and an ingrained reluctance to speak the truth and set things straight. Smiling on the outside, hurting on the inside, even simple eye contact too uncomfortable to maintain. <br /><br />This author has a knack for writing characters so vivid that you would recognize any one of them if he or she came knocking at your front doo</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11584281845065376755"><em>'. . . just one more dropped stitch in a family tapestry full of holes.'</em><br /><br />This is a story of strong familial bonds forged early in life, then eroding from deep-seated resentments, guilt, and an ingrained reluctance to speak the truth and set things straight. Smiling on the outside, hurting on the inside, even simple eye contact too uncomfortable to maintain. <br /><br />This author has a knack for writing characters so vivid that you would recognize any one of them if he or she came knocking at your front door. Luke, Matt, Katie, and Bo Morrison, orphaned early in the novel, are struggling to make a life without their parents. I can still almost hear baby Bo clanging those pots and pans playing on the floor while big brother Matt tries to study at the kitchen table. You will make the acquaintance of their neighbors, the tragic Pye family, with their messy lives and mean secrets. I cannot say enough about the author's skill with character development. Major and minor players alike are fashioned with care, and I reference old Miss Vernon, she of the rattling teeth and long whiskery jaw, 100 years old and a most extraordinary storyteller. <br /><br />Small, quiet, and unassuming, this story will stay with me for a bit. Excellent.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11584281845065376755">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_931915310">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/931915310" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3845138-carol" class="left imgcol" title="Carol"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1438649960p2/3845138.jpg" alt="Carol" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/931915310?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 29, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3845138-carol" class="user" title="Carol">Carol</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer931915310">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9954925564650166221">This was a simply told and captivating family drama with a northern Ontario setting. I’ve never been there but the author’s observations of that area in Canada established such a powerful sense of place that the story came alive for me. I grew up in the high plains of eastern Colorado (Kent Haruf “territory”) and there were parts of this novel that echo my own rural background – especially her description of life for farm families.<br /><br />The story details the struggles of four siblings who were orphane</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9954925564650166221">This was a simply told and captivating family drama with a northern Ontario setting. I’ve never been there but the author’s observations of that area in Canada established such a powerful sense of place that the story came alive for me. I grew up in the high plains of eastern Colorado (Kent Haruf “territory”) and there were parts of this novel that echo my own rural background – especially her description of life for farm families.<br /><br />The story details the struggles of four siblings who were orphaned when their parents are killed. It’s told through the eyes of the adult sister, Kate, who was seven years old at the time of the deadly car accident. She reflects on her childhood as she reluctantly prepares to return for a family gathering. Her account slowly reveals details of the broken dreams and sacrifices that they all made in their efforts to keep the family together. Nothing very dramatic happens yet it elegantly portrays events that affect most families – hopeful aspirations, misunderstandings, missed chances and sibling rivalry.<br /><br />I have several brothers and sisters of my own so parts of this story provoked reflections of my own life. I thoroughly enjoyed this finely crafted and bittersweet tale of survival and love of family. <br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9954925564650166221">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_982182340">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/982182340" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/9246169-wanda" class="left imgcol" title="Wanda"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1441213786p2/9246169.jpg" alt="Wanda" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/982182340?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 21, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/9246169-wanda" class="user" title="Wanda">Wanda</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=canadian-eh" class="actionLinkLite">canadian-eh</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=favourites" class="actionLinkLite">favourites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=female-authors" class="actionLinkLite">female-authors</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=cbc-100-cdn-novels" class="actionLinkLite">cbc-100-cdn-novels</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=read-in-2014" class="actionLinkLite">read-in-2014</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer982182340">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15539578866992528356">I feel like I’ve just been hit by a truck—this book blindsided me and despite the impact, I adore it. This may be because it ticks so many of my personal boxes, but I’ve been wandering the house since I finished it, ploughing my way through laundry, dinner, dishes, trying desperately to find my footing again, while I’m processing.<br /><br />Within the first few pages of the book, Katherine’s parents are killed in a car accident, sending the four children on a confusing, agonizing struggle to put their live</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15539578866992528356">I feel like I’ve just been hit by a truck—this book blindsided me and despite the impact, I adore it. This may be because it ticks so many of my personal boxes, but I’ve been wandering the house since I finished it, ploughing my way through laundry, dinner, dishes, trying desperately to find my footing again, while I’m processing.<br /><br />Within the first few pages of the book, Katherine’s parents are killed in a car accident, sending the four children on a confusing, agonizing struggle to put their lives back together again. Katherine is only seven years old—I was 34 when it happened to me, and my life was blown apart and has never fully recovered. <br /><br />“You make it sound like it was centuries ago,” Daniel said. “If you parents died when you were seven, it’s barely twenty years.”<br />“It feels like centuries,” I said.<br /><br /> Lawson nails it with that tiny bit of dialog. Although it’s been 18 years since my parents’ car accident, some days it feels like yesterday—other days it feels like I never had parents. And I completely relate to Katherine’s numbness, the reluctance to feel anything about anyone—if you care, there’s a good chance that they will get yanked away from you. Not caring seems like your only defence against heart wrenching pain. The only problem is that is doesn’t work. People like Katherine’s boyfriend Daniel worm their way into your life and you reluctantly begin to care about them, all the while struggling to see them as temporary and frustrating the hell out of them, as they wonder what is wrong with you.<br /><br />I clearly remember the day that I put my emotions on ice—it was about a year and a half after the funerals and I remember thinking, “I’m so tired of crying.” So I quit. It has taken years to thaw that permafrost and I’m still unsure that the process is finished. Still a bit freezer-burned, I guess.<br /><br />It’s taken me eighteen years, but I’ve finally been able to engage with my family again—they’ve been very patient, they waited and I’ve been accepted back without reservation. Knowing this makes me love them fiercely—after being emotionally frozen for so many years, the strength of that love surprises me on each and every occasion that I spend time with them.<br /><br />I also have a farm background like Katherine and used university as a way to do something different—I even started my university career as a biology major until I was seduced by so many other interesting subjects and wandered away into the arts and social sciences. But I have so many fond memories of wandering the coulees of home, identifying wild flowers, scooping snails out of the pond, and studying the ground squirrels as I emulated my personal idol, Jane Goodall [chimpanzees were in short supply, but ground squirrels were plentiful on the prairies].<br /><br />So I may have been predisposed to love this book—still, I cannot recommend it highly enough. It is worth more than 5 stars to me.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15539578866992528356">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_64631857">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/64631857" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1976848-jane-siviski" class="left imgcol" title="Jane Siviski"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1233370915p2/1976848.jpg" alt="Jane Siviski" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/64631857?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 24, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1976848-jane-siviski" class="user" title="Jane Siviski">Jane Siviski</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer64631857">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12326069984152178226">It's a strange thing that I came across this book. I found it wedged into the back of a shelf downstairs. It seems I read it at the perfect time. It came to me in the midst of serious, physical grief, the kind where your body is taken over by sadness and is simply a vessel for your shaking and sobbing and wailing. Oh the wailing. You will wail. And not in the Wayne's World sense. Its the kind of thing that you can't let anyone else listen to, because what the wailing is is the pain leaving your</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12326069984152178226">It's a strange thing that I came across this book. I found it wedged into the back of a shelf downstairs. It seems I read it at the perfect time. It came to me in the midst of serious, physical grief, the kind where your body is taken over by sadness and is simply a vessel for your shaking and sobbing and wailing. Oh the wailing. You will wail. And not in the Wayne's World sense. Its the kind of thing that you can't let anyone else listen to, because what the wailing is is the pain leaving your body. That pain, floating about untethered, can do serious injury. That's why you do it alone. <br /><br />So the same day I'm holding my cousin's 14 hour old baby in my arms, and the knowledge that my own mother won't be there when I hold my children in my arms for the first time- or any time- comes searing through my blood, I find this book. <br /><br />Kate's parents are both killed in a car crash when she is seven years old, on a day that had begun like any other. As an adult, Kate returns to the year following her parents death, when, to prevent the family from being broken apart more, Luke, the oldest, then 19, decides to raise Matt, 17, Kate ,7, and Bo, 1, on his own. <br /><br />Perhaps it is hardest for Kate. Losing both parents at seven years old. Such a vulnerable age. Her fear is palpable. If one of her brothers is late coming home, she is certain, until she sees his face, that he is dead too. It's heartbreaking. When you think about it, how often do you get a window into a grieving seven year-old? Though the book is fiction, it has the vividness of an autobiography. <br /><br /><br />Amid the complication, the arguing, and the desperation among them all, love is present: it is clear and pervasive and strong, and nothing short of miraculous. While this poor child is swallowed by fear, she loves her brothers. And they love her. Grief is a lifelong plague- but the love she has for her family is continually extricating her from it, even into adulthood. Its a fascinating look at time. It is fascinating to see what she carries with her- because its all the right stuff. <br /><br />As a younger sister, as a victim of parental loss, as someone who is seeking to define herself as an adult in the face of grief, and as someone who has a family (albeit a broken one) I found this book to be enormously powerful, perhaps because it is so relevant. But the writing is simple and beautiful. It is a stunning portrait of familial love (we take its potency for granted.) The story is phenomenally complex but equally simple. Love simplifies things. By the end of the book I was struck dumb, totally overcome with one truth the story brought to light: No love is ordinary. <br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12326069984152178226">...more</a>

          </span>

  <a href="/user/show/10592271-brokentune" class="left imgcol" title="BrokenTune"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1391017756p2/10592271.jpg" alt="BrokenTune" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1143658450?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 06, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/10592271-brokentune" class="user" title="BrokenTune">BrokenTune</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/7175894-crow-lake" title="Paperback 9780701173449" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/10592271-brokentune?shelf=reviewed" class="actionLinkLite">reviewed</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/10592271-brokentune?shelf=canada" class="actionLinkLite">canada</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1143658450">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7635087353128248783"><em>"I remember reading somewhere a theory to the effect that each member of a family has a role – ‘the clever one’, ‘the pretty one’, ‘the selfish one ’. Once you’ve been established in the role for a while you’re stuck with it – no matter what you do people will still see you as whatever-it-was – but in the early stages, according to the theory, you have some choice as to what your role will be."</em><br /><br />This was one of the books on my "Canada" reading list. Most titles on this list are books and authors I</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7635087353128248783"><em>"I remember reading somewhere a theory to the effect that each member of a family has a role – ‘the clever one’, ‘the pretty one’, ‘the selfish one ’. Once you’ve been established in the role for a while you’re stuck with it – no matter what you do people will still see you as whatever-it-was – but in the early stages, according to the theory, you have some choice as to what your role will be."</em><br /><br />This was one of the books on my "Canada" reading list. Most titles on this list are books and authors I had never heard of before setting myself a challenge to read up on the place I'm going to visit this summer. So, I knew nothing of Crow Lake before I started reading. Unfortunately, I also read parts of the Ferguson brothers' How to be a Canadian at the same time - and the Ferguson's acerbic description of what makes the quintessential Canadian novel seemed to very much apply to Crow Lake.<br /><br /><em>"Understatement was the rule in our house. Emotions, even positive ones, were kept firmly under control. It was the Eleventh Commandment, carved on its very own tablet of stone and presented specifically to those of Presbyterian persuasion: Thou Shalt Not Emote."</em><br /><br />Don't get me wrong, Crow Lake has got a great premise and interesting characters but the dysfunction and hardship described seemed rather prefabricated. <br />I also found it hard to relate to Kate, the main character, who is raised by her brothers after their parents die in an accident. <br />But part of me is convinced that it is hard to relate to Kate for anyone because she does not relate to anyone in the book. In fact, one of the issues dealt with in the book is the emotional detachment which people create for themselves as protection against loss.<br /><br />Another aspect of this detachment, and one which also did not help to endear Kate to me, was her determination to not just shut out her family, but also to use the acquisition of knowledge as an escape mechanism.<br /><br />All in all, it was an interesting book, but the aloofness (in generous terms) of the main character made it sometimes hard work to want to work with the story and see the characters open up about their lives. <br /><br /><em>"Great Grandmother Morrison, I accept that the fault is largely mine, but I do hold you partly to blame. It is you, with your love of learning, who set the standard against which I have judged everyone around me, all of my life. I have pursued your dream single-mindedly; I have become familiar with books and ideas you never even imagined, and somehow, in the process of acquiring all that knowledge , I have managed to learn nothing at all." </em></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7635087353128248783">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_77783582">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/77783582" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/666804-terry" class="left imgcol" title="Terry"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1451845281p2/666804.jpg" alt="Terry" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/77783582?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 14, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/666804-terry" class="user" title="Terry">Terry</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/666804-terry?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer77783582">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11771836701544525501">My roommate means well but boy does she have bland taste in "literature" and yes I say "literature" with as much sarcasm as possible. Believe me I turn down more offerings of hers than I read, but I just feel obligated to choke one or two down every once in a while. <br /><br />I wanted to like it, I did. I just didn't. It just didn't feel "new" to me. And I didn't find the writing style especially evocative. The narrator of the story speaks and thinks like a 50+-year-old, and I kept shaking my head to clea</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11771836701544525501">My roommate means well but boy does she have bland taste in "literature" and yes I say "literature" with as much sarcasm as possible. Believe me I turn down more offerings of hers than I read, but I just feel obligated to choke one or two down every once in a while. <br /><br />I wanted to like it, I did. I just didn't. It just didn't feel "new" to me. And I didn't find the writing style especially evocative. The narrator of the story speaks and thinks like a 50+-year-old, and I kept shaking my head to clear it when I was reminded that she was in her late 20s. <br /><br />And I REALLY. REALLY. REALLY. hate books that keep "the big moment!!!!!!!" until the last few pages, but spend the previous 300+ pages muttering dark asides such as "But that was before...it...happened" or "If I only knew what was about to happen, I wouldn't have stayed" or "He didn't realize his life was about to change" or variations upon that theme, so that "the big moment!!!!!!!!" ends up feeling anticlimactic at best. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11771836701544525501">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer3302110470210177364"><br />This story is about much more than relationships. It is about life. It is about organisms that live in ponds and how they cling to life. It is about family. It is about carrying on the dreams and hopes of previous generations. It is about education and how important it is and how, sometimes, you miss life as it is happening. It is a lyrical and wonderfully magical story that ensnares you in its telling. It is dreamy and haunting. It is one of the better books I've read this year ~~ it is somethi</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3302110470210177364"><br />This story is about much more than relationships. It is about life. It is about organisms that live in ponds and how they cling to life. It is about family. It is about carrying on the dreams and hopes of previous generations. It is about education and how important it is and how, sometimes, you miss life as it is happening. It is a lyrical and wonderfully magical story that ensnares you in its telling. It is dreamy and haunting. It is one of the better books I've read this year ~~ it is something I was not expecting to read and, now, I wish there was more of it.<br /><br />There is nothing in this book that seemed out of place or false.<br /><br />It is said that all good writing must provide a 'sense of place' in which the action occurs, and Crow Lake and the surrounding community are almost as important as characters as are the humans in this lyrical and evocative book.<br /><br />Crow Lake succeeds on all levels.</span>

I've been chewing on this book for a couple of days now, never being really invested in it to make a big to finishing it, certainly not devouring it like the last five books or so.   <span style="display:none" id="freeText3018788363504390459">Book #26 of 2009<br />Back to the serious side of things I guess... I've been chewing on this book for a couple of days now, never being really invested in it to make a big to finishing it, certainly not devouring it like the last five books or so. I'm not even really sure how I feel about it. Its almost anticlimatic in a way. The whole book builds up to this supposedly huge catastrophe, which isn't really a catastrophe at all, but a decision moving life one way instead of another. But the supposed catastrophe isn't even really the point of the book, it's the narrator's view of said catastrophe that is really the purpose of the book it seems, and the climax is such a small mental shift in the way she looks at "the catastrophe". The narrator herself isn't really an overly likeable character in my opinion. When she talks about her younger self she just seems so selfish. But then again, she was a kid so I guess that is to be expected. And when she talks about her adult self, she seems so aloof, emotionally univnested in much of anything. </span>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17240603791247471793">One way to tell if I have enjoyed a book is as I near the end I purposely slow my reading down. I don't want my relationship with the characters to end I want to savor the final knowing..... <br /><br />Such was the case with Crow lake. Kate is an academic from a rural Northern Canadian small town. At 27 she is falling in love with Daniel,a fellow academic who has less complicated background. For her to come to terms with this, she needs to gain insight into why it is not easy to let love in. Her past contains tragedy, sensitivity, acts of unselfish kindness and harsh brutality, all of which she has not really examined. She is closely linked to her three siblings and their shared experiences but has managed to compartmentalize things and perhaps have a different view<br /><br />The story is a delicate unfolding of her self discovery towards a closer perception of the shared view. </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1099670582">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1099670582" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1899836-snotchocheez" class="left imgcol" title="Snotchocheez"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1428104712p2/1899836.jpg" alt="Snotchocheez" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1099670582?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 09, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1899836-snotchocheez" class="user" title="Snotchocheez">Snotchocheez</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1099670582">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3181967659084120938"><strong>3.5 stars</strong><br /><br />Spare and somber (but not overly so), Mary Lawson's <em>Crow Lake</em> is evocative of Carol Shields' Pulitzer Prize-winning novel <em>The Stone Diaries</em> but (to its credit) isn't quite as overladen with flowery prose (or twee cutesiness). Both novels are set in rural Canada (<em>Crow Lake</em> in the northern-most inhabited reaches of Ontario, <em>Stone Diaries</em> in stone quarry-filled central Manitoba); both feature a female narrator whistfully looking back on their hardscrabble rural pasts. In the right melancho</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3181967659084120938"><strong>3.5 stars</strong><br /><br />Spare and somber (but not overly so), Mary Lawson's <em>Crow Lake</em> is evocative of Carol Shields' Pulitzer Prize-winning novel <em>The Stone Diaries</em> but (to its credit) isn't quite as overladen with flowery prose (or twee cutesiness). Both novels are set in rural Canada (<em>Crow Lake</em> in the northern-most inhabited reaches of Ontario, <em>Stone Diaries</em> in stone quarry-filled central Manitoba); both feature a female narrator whistfully looking back on their hardscrabble rural pasts. In the right melancholic frame of mind, I enjoyed both of these, but <em>Crow Lake</em> ultimately wins out with a more relatable story.<br /><br />Kate Morrison, twenty-eight year-old environmental biology professor at an unnamed (but probably Toronto-based) university, reminisces about her life in Crow Lake, a sugar beet farming community with little more than a schoolhouse, a general store, and a Presbyterian church to barely mark it on the map. She focues back to when she was eight years-old, on the two years following the tragic deaths of her parents, and how she and her three siblings (two much older teen brothers Matt and Luke, and infant sister Bo) try to keep what remains of their family together. The Morrison family, despite living in a farming community that prioritized (by necessity) crop returns for survival's sake, valued education above all. So the elder Morrison boys, university-bound, are faced with the decision of either shelving their academic dreams (and working the fields, an Herculean task when you've got two young siblings to take care of) or submitting to the wishes of distant family willing to take them in (but in doing so breaking up the sibling bonds that tie them together.)<br /><br />This is not a particularly splashy novel, but it is quietly affecting, and not nearly as depressing as the jacket info led me<br />to imagine. I believe this was Ms. Lawson's first novel; I'd like to visit her more recent works as I'm plenty certain she's got more lovely stories of rural Ontario life and family to share with us.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3181967659084120938">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer127494304">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7064782262637647743">This novel is very much like consuming several empty calories: so much to read, not a lot to comprehend. This novel went on, and on, and on. I found a lot of the information given about Katie's past overly descriptive. Far too much irrelevant detail! I also found the book to be quite repetitive, I don't know how many times it was mentioned that the neighbors were dropping off meals. I don't know how many uncomfortable dinner table conversations there were. I don't know how many chapters were ded</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7064782262637647743">This novel is very much like consuming several empty calories: so much to read, not a lot to comprehend. This novel went on, and on, and on. I found a lot of the information given about Katie's past overly descriptive. Far too much irrelevant detail! I also found the book to be quite repetitive, I don't know how many times it was mentioned that the neighbors were dropping off meals. I don't know how many uncomfortable dinner table conversations there were. I don't know how many chapters were dedicated to Bo's common needs, changing the diaper, putting her to bed, etc.<br />I found Katie to be quite frustrating. She's insanely negative! She's also over dramatic. It bothered me the fact that she was still rolling around in her own pity at age 30. It think she's pathetic, to be focusing SO much on the past, and letting it affect her present life. I never found it overly clear why she looked up to Matt so much. Yeah, he's brainy and I get that he spent time with her at the ponds, but that was about it. There was no specific event that made her glorified perspective of matt all that understandable. She even mentioned that "Daniel just isn't Matt." It bothered me that here she was, 30 years old, studying what she loved, teaching it to other students, and in a serious relationship with a sensible man, and she's unhappy. It drives me insane. <br />This book was like reading some crazy lady's every thought. Terrible novel. If I could give this book a half a star I would. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7064782262637647743">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_376549442">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/376549442" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/9192013-sonya" class="left imgcol" title="Sonya"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1335877010p2/9192013.jpg" alt="Sonya" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/376549442?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 25, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/9192013-sonya" class="user" title="Sonya">Sonya</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer376549442">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15733512276391000163">This book has been sitting on my shelf, unread, for many years. I wish I'd left it there. While this is Mary Lawson's first book, my sympathy is limited. She offers no depth to her characters. You develop no bonds with anyone in the book. It has a poorly assembled storyline which is supposed to develop into an emotional epiphany for the flat protagonist "Kate"; however, by the end of the book, Kate has learned almost nothing about herself and, what she has "learned", was not picked up by her aft</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15733512276391000163">This book has been sitting on my shelf, unread, for many years. I wish I'd left it there. While this is Mary Lawson's first book, my sympathy is limited. She offers no depth to her characters. You develop no bonds with anyone in the book. It has a poorly assembled storyline which is supposed to develop into an emotional epiphany for the flat protagonist "Kate"; however, by the end of the book, Kate has learned almost nothing about herself and, what she has "learned", was not picked up by her after years of emotional self-probing, but laid out plainly for her in speeches by other characters in the story. She refuses to accept that they might be right and, thus, nothing has changed for her by the end of the story, which Lawson sloppily wraps up in the last few pages.<br /><br />While some of the characters in the book are interesting, none display more than one defining feature. You grow to dislike the protagonist almost immediately and her little sister "Bo" is nothing but a ball of anger intended to make the protagonist appear less annoying. While the storyline had hope, originally, the author is clearly unskilled and unfit to develop it into something meaningful. She repeats herself on obvious points about the protagonist's fear of emotional bonding and she makes a passing reference to the Luke's possible homosexuality in a side comment that is never mentioned again. Too bad, since he's one of the characters I actually liked.<br /><br />I did appreciate the author's description of life for farming families in early northern Ontario. It made me want to re-read some Robertson Davies. However, if a story of hard life for Canadian pioneering families in the early 1900s is what you seek, pick up some Margaret Laurence or Robertson Davies to read it done well. This book is considered "Canadian Lit" because it takes place in northern Ontario and the author was born here and probably still has her Canadian passport tucked away in a shoebox with some other trinkets from her childhood. Mary Lawson has clearly not been to northern Ontario since she was a child and has no real memory of it except for insects and water; she wrote the book in Britain where she has lived since she was a teenager.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15733512276391000163">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/20048096-pamela?shelf=top-picks-read-in-2013-2014" class="actionLinkLite">top-picks-read-in-2013-2014</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/20048096-pamela?shelf=canada" class="actionLinkLite">canada</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/20048096-pamela?shelf=family" class="actionLinkLite">family</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/20048096-pamela?shelf=contemporary" class="actionLinkLite">contemporary</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/20048096-pamela?shelf=rivers-and-lakes" class="actionLinkLite">rivers-and-lakes</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/20048096-pamela?shelf=own" class="actionLinkLite">own</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1003835348">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2389658394350049676">A caterpillar, within a cocoon over a set period of time, transforms into a butterfly. But it is the struggle to emerge which allows it to thrive, transformed. Figuratively, humans who have experienced tragedies are like caterpillars; except that the cocoon period varies from individual to individual, as does the degree of struggle required to resurrect anew from the experience. In that sense, tragedy can be a beautiful miraculous thing. <br /><br />Mary Lawson, in her novel “Crow Lake,” not only weaves a b</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2389658394350049676">A caterpillar, within a cocoon over a set period of time, transforms into a butterfly. But it is the struggle to emerge which allows it to thrive, transformed. Figuratively, humans who have experienced tragedies are like caterpillars; except that the cocoon period varies from individual to individual, as does the degree of struggle required to resurrect anew from the experience. In that sense, tragedy can be a beautiful miraculous thing. <br /><br />Mary Lawson, in her novel “Crow Lake,” not only weaves a beautiful story of tragedy and transformation she does so with an impassioned pen and with a deep understanding of nuances, frailties, strengths and imperfections of the human experience. <br /><br />The Morrison family along with all other major/minor characters are well-developed, readily identifiable and ultra realistic. From their mannerisms to personality traits to speech patterns to physical attributes, nothing felt contrived or artificial; everything was purposeful and complementary to the story. And the atmospheric setting - “the wild terrain of northern Ontario” – mirrored in the beautiful, hauntingly stark book cover, adds paralleling depth and contrast to this dramatic novel. A novel I’m delighted to have purchased because it touched me deeply and I can reread and cherish it for years to come.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2389658394350049676">...more</a>

          </span>

              <a href="/review/list/1246837-maggie-stiefvater?shelf=recommended" class="actionLinkLite">recommended</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer24749263">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7585798262796007973">It took me a month to read Crow Lake from beginning to end. Not because it's a long book, but because it's a slow book. It's a story of two girls raised by their older brothers after their parents die in a car crash. It portrays, poignantly and humorously, the close relationship between the siblings -- absolutely beautiful. The plot will not change the world, but by the end of the book, I felt like I'd met four people and I was sad to leave them. I'd love to accomplish that in my writing.<br /><br /><br /><br />***wond</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/45284161?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 03, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1990033-lisa" class="user" title="Lisa">Lisa</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer45284161">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17540455639254484026">First novels are tough. Many haven't mastered the art of "show, don't tell" in their story-telling. Mary Lawson does a beautiful job of getting you to feel the emotions of the characters and their varying reactions to the tragedy that occurs near the beginning of the book. This is especially hard, since the setting is a small farming community where you are not supposed to show your emotions. You are supposed to be stoic in face of anything and everything, although stoicism can easily lead to fe</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17540455639254484026">First novels are tough. Many haven't mastered the art of "show, don't tell" in their story-telling. Mary Lawson does a beautiful job of getting you to feel the emotions of the characters and their varying reactions to the tragedy that occurs near the beginning of the book. This is especially hard, since the setting is a small farming community where you are not supposed to show your emotions. You are supposed to be stoic in face of anything and everything, although stoicism can easily lead to feelings of martyrdom.<br /><br />Perhaps this novel captivated me, because I grew up in a household where words of anger were not spoken, but certainly the anger was there. She described very well what it's like to swallow that anger and keep it down throughout your life.<br /><br />The ending is quiet, and will disappoint those who want some large explosive fanfare ending. But I found it very intense, even it its quiet realization. It's a fairly quick read and will be of interest to those who like to know what makes people tick.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_47528151">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/47528151" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1524767-poiema" class="left imgcol" title="Poiema"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1352414613p2/1524767.jpg" alt="Poiema" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/47528151?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 27, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1524767-poiema" class="user" title="Poiema">Poiema</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Wendell Berry, Mitford book fans</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Poiema by: </span>
      <a rel="nofollow" href="http://ukrakovianki.blogspot.com/" target="_blank">http://ukrakovianki.blogspot.com/</a>
  </div>



    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer47528151">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4304561260391827074">I couldn't put this book down until I finished @ 3:30 a.m. It's been a while since I have done that but I'd have to say Crow Lake was worth it.<br /><br />The narrator, Kate, is a 27 year old biologist with a very successful career. But that career does not fully satisfy her because she has loose ends to tidy up with her siblings. It's something that she would rather postpone indefinitely, but circumstances force her to sort through the emotional baggage. Her story builds to a satisfactory conclusion and sh</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4304561260391827074">I couldn't put this book down until I finished @ 3:30 a.m. It's been a while since I have done that but I'd have to say Crow Lake was worth it.<br /><br />The narrator, Kate, is a 27 year old biologist with a very successful career. But that career does not fully satisfy her because she has loose ends to tidy up with her siblings. It's something that she would rather postpone indefinitely, but circumstances force her to sort through the emotional baggage. Her story builds to a satisfactory conclusion and she is able to at least begin to bridge the emotional gaps between herself and her loved ones.<br /><br />When she was 7, Kate's parents both died in a car accident. Her two older brothers, still teens themselves, take on the responsibility of raising Kate and her baby sister. Engaged in rebuilding their family life, the latent personality traits of each family member comes to the surface. Their struggles are shared as a family unit, but are at the same time personal battles that shape their futures forever.<br /><br />Intertwined as a subplot is the extremely tragic tale of a neighboring family whose lives intersect with those of Kate and her siblings. At first we see these people only from a distance, but layers of their story unfold and become the shared history of both families together.<br /><br />Especially interesting to me is that Mary Lawson explores the way that family members "harden" their perceptions of one another. As adults we tend to forget that our siblings have added dimensions to their personalities on beyond those we shared in the foundational stages of our lives. At some point it can only be healthy to shatter the old lens and take a fresh look at siblings as they are in the present.<br /><br />The setting of this novel was so well drawn. It took place in the Lake Country of Ontario and was viewed through the eyes of the budding biologist, Kate. The descriptions of the lake and the insects and the seasons were lush and verdant. <br /><br />A great novel. Highly recommended.<br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4304561260391827074">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_257070534">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/257070534" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/374653-judy" class="left imgcol" title="Judy"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1189966224p2/374653.jpg" alt="Judy" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/257070534?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 15, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/374653-judy" class="user" title="Judy">Judy</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/374653-judy?shelf=bookgroup" class="actionLinkLite">bookgroup</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/374653-judy?shelf=novel" class="actionLinkLite">novel</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/374653-judy?shelf=small-town-character-driven" class="actionLinkLite">small-town-character-driven</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer257070534">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9620987191763292436">January 5, 2012<br />4.5 This is a beautiful, rich novel marbled with huge swaths of sadness, set in a town in the open spaces of northern Ontario. It is so simple, and yet so richly layered, that I have trouble describing it: it's about a family, as well as some other families in their small town. There is a traumatic event - really a number of them. The families go on. The narrator, a member of the central family, is looking back at these events, which are interwoven with scenes from the present. My</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9620987191763292436">January 5, 2012<br />4.5 This is a beautiful, rich novel marbled with huge swaths of sadness, set in a town in the open spaces of northern Ontario. It is so simple, and yet so richly layered, that I have trouble describing it: it's about a family, as well as some other families in their small town. There is a traumatic event - really a number of them. The families go on. The narrator, a member of the central family, is looking back at these events, which are interwoven with scenes from the present. My only quarrel (quibble) is with the ending, in which enlightenment seems to come too fast and too neatly. However, the power and truth of the rest of the novel far outweighs that.<br /><br />I realize that I have read a lot of books lately that fall into this same category: the internal lives of family in a small town in the wilderness (the novels of Kent Haruf; <em>Out Stealing Horses</em>; <em>Amy and Isabelle</em> and others by Elizabeth Strout; <em>The Bird Sisters</em>). Is this a genre/sub-genre? In any case, the books I have read, although alike in this way, are completely individual, based as they are on the people and place they focus on with such a keen lens.<br /><br />November 29, 2012<br />We read this for book group this month, and I was struck anew by the beauty of the story - so profound, and yet so simple.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9620987191763292436">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_767665069">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/767665069" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4987113-lori" class="left imgcol" title="Lori"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1377388557p2/4987113.jpg" alt="Lori" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/767665069?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 10, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4987113-lori" class="user" title="Lori">Lori</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer767665069">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15308562473486084876">3.5 stars. Mary Lawson's Crow Lake is identified as 1 of the 100 must-read Canadian books. I had read her second novel,The Other Side of the Bridge, quite some time ago and thought it was excellent. I had always meant to go back to Crow Lake and finally did. Although not quite as well-developed and poignant as "Bridge", at least in my opinion, this is a very enjoyable book. Kate, at 27, has been invited to her nephew's 18th birthday back in her hometown of Crow Lake and the invitation, along wit</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15308562473486084876">3.5 stars. Mary Lawson's Crow Lake is identified as 1 of the 100 must-read Canadian books. I had read her second novel,The Other Side of the Bridge, quite some time ago and thought it was excellent. I had always meant to go back to Crow Lake and finally did. Although not quite as well-developed and poignant as "Bridge", at least in my opinion, this is a very enjoyable book. Kate, at 27, has been invited to her nephew's 18th birthday back in her hometown of Crow Lake and the invitation, along with Kate's budding relationship with a fellow professor, has spurred Kate into reflection on her past and how it has so affected her present both her current relationship with Daniel and her ability to emote and open up about her family and her past. Kate's parents died tragically in a car crash when Kate was 7 and she and her 1-year old sister Bo and 17- year old brother, Matt, were raised by their brother Luke, barely 19 at the time. The lives of this family, both the joyful and the tragic, are gradually revealed through Kate's recollections. Also spotlighted is the personality, culture and beauty of the northern Ontario farming community where they grew up. The community spirit, the support provided these children by neighbours, the doctor, the minister and the schoolteacher, highlights so clearly how it really does take a community to raise a child. The beauty of this book is how quietly and subtly it unfolds the story of the Morrison!s and their closest neighbour, the Pye's. Without being hit over the head with it, the reader joins Kate in her journey from emotional constipation to realization and awakening. The comparably sad and tragic situation of the Pye's, highlights the key ingredient to family survival and resilience, unconditional love. This is apparent within the Morrison family(and so mournfully lacking in the Pye family) and is what Kate realizes ultimately is all that' she needs to trust in to wholly reconnect with and embrace her siblings for who they are and equally important, fully connect with her lover, Daniel and her current life. A recommended read.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15308562473486084876">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/660689852" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2776022-eden-arielle" class="left imgcol" title="Eden Arielle"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" alt="Eden Arielle" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/660689852?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 06, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2776022-eden-arielle" class="user" title="Eden Arielle">Eden Arielle</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer660689852">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15727391770031477307">I picked up this book with low expectations, but here is a novel so beautiful and intricate in its examination of humanity that I couldn't help but love it. In addition to its calm, steady depiction of family, it is also surprisingly fast-paced and thrilling and I devoured it in a span of two days, fully engrossed in the lives of the characters.<br />The story follows Kate, a now-college professor looking back on her childhood in northern Ontario. Affected deeply by tragedy at a young age, the story m</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15727391770031477307">I picked up this book with low expectations, but here is a novel so beautiful and intricate in its examination of humanity that I couldn't help but love it. In addition to its calm, steady depiction of family, it is also surprisingly fast-paced and thrilling and I devoured it in a span of two days, fully engrossed in the lives of the characters.<br />The story follows Kate, a now-college professor looking back on her childhood in northern Ontario. Affected deeply by tragedy at a young age, the story mostly focuses on her and her siblings as they try to struggle through in the days and months following the calamity. What makes this book beautiful is its description of human empathy. <br />I was particularly fascinated by the character Matt and discovered many similarities between myself and the main character, especially in her view of him, even following her thought process and being as moved as she was in the end. I typically dislike anti-climactic endings, but this is a book unflagging in its depiction of the real world and its lack of glamor. I found the ending to be satisfying and emotional.<br />Although the book has its flaws, especially involving certain aspects of the later Kate, I would highly recommend it. It is beautifully written and perfectly structured, a wonderful first novel that I found myself thinking about long after I closed it.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15727391770031477307">...more</a>

          </span>

  <a href="/user/show/7487619-rachel-theshadesoforange" class="left imgcol" title="Rachel (TheShadesofOrange)"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1429330875p2/7487619.jpg" alt="Rachel (TheShadesofOrange)" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1204871895?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 24, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/7487619-rachel-theshadesoforange" class="user" title="Rachel (TheShadesofOrange)">Rachel (TheShadesofOrange)</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/7487619-rachel-theshadesoforange?shelf=general-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">general-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1204871895">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer953065989382231485"><strong>4.5 Stars - Such a Beautiful Story</strong><br /><br />There are two reasons I loved this novel: the characters &amp; the setting. <br /><br />First, the characters were fleshed out and fully developed. Complex and lovable, the Morrison children felt like real people. I don't usually get attached to characters in novel, but I found myself caring deeply for each of the main characters. (I adored Bo!)<br /><br />Second, the setting was incredibly well drawn. Lawson writes such an authentic experience of living in a northern Canadian communit</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText953065989382231485"><strong>4.5 Stars - Such a Beautiful Story</strong><br /><br />There are two reasons I loved this novel: the characters &amp; the setting. <br /><br />First, the characters were fleshed out and fully developed. Complex and lovable, the Morrison children felt like real people. I don't usually get attached to characters in novel, but I found myself caring deeply for each of the main characters. (I adored Bo!)<br /><br />Second, the setting was incredibly well drawn. Lawson writes such an authentic experience of living in a northern Canadian community. I loved reading her rich descriptions of the natural beauty. She perfectly described the contradictory nature of Northern Canada, both incredibly isolating and rich with community. <br /><br />The language is very accessible, yet still lovely. Lawson plays with so many themes including family, education, regret, and pride to create a simple, thought-provoking story. I would highly recommend this wonderful piece of Canadian literature. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="953065989382231485">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer1662776800286870411">I chose this book for our June book club. It was a “Today Show” book choice. Told from the perspective of 27 year old Kate, the story is one of sadness (her parents died in a car crash 20 years earlier), forgiveness, guilt and growth. I enjoyed the story and especially the flaws that Kate has as a character and person. As a reader it was rewarding to witness her evolution as a character throughout the narrative. Our book club had some good discussion and most (though not all) liked the book. Mos</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1662776800286870411">I chose this book for our June book club. It was a “Today Show” book choice. Told from the perspective of 27 year old Kate, the story is one of sadness (her parents died in a car crash 20 years earlier), forgiveness, guilt and growth. I enjoyed the story and especially the flaws that Kate has as a character and person. As a reader it was rewarding to witness her evolution as a character throughout the narrative. Our book club had some good discussion and most (though not all) liked the book. Most agreed the beginning was a bit slow but it rapidly picked up 60 pages or so into the novel.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1037545760">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1174792281873473474">This author's work I have read quite out of order. But I liked this one much better than "Road Ends". It's 4.5 star level in its characterizations all around. Yes, my favorite of this Northern Ontario locale of hers that I have read. We know 3 or 4 families well at Crow Lake this time, but we know the narrator's family the most deeply. Smart people, and some like Matt are still their own worst enemies in the anxiety their smarts seem to inflame about their future. <br /><br />Kate is not my favorite class o</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1174792281873473474">This author's work I have read quite out of order. But I liked this one much better than "Road Ends". It's 4.5 star level in its characterizations all around. Yes, my favorite of this Northern Ontario locale of hers that I have read. We know 3 or 4 families well at Crow Lake this time, but we know the narrator's family the most deeply. Smart people, and some like Matt are still their own worst enemies in the anxiety their smarts seem to inflame about their future. <br /><br />Kate is not my favorite class of narrator/protagonist (rather a whiner and selfish to the bone core), but we are lead to an understanding of what it looks like from her perspective. Luke was drawn so well, and Bo was captured in words just superbly. <br /><br />This isn't a happy tale, and there are multiple types of dysfunction and suffering. And yet this book was not a downer over all. There were two or three people that kept it from being far more than just a conceited tale of "how I made good while the others blew it" junket that you get all the time now in the moderns. As if financial success and educational superiority tips all the scales for self-satisfaction. <br /><br />I loved the way this one ended, with some realizations to rock the insular reserve. There was much to think about after reading this book. Especially about what neighbors mean to people in different places and different time periods. I would like to discuss this one in a book group with Returning Adult program college students with a medium age of 48-52, or in that category of schooling, even if not in the older bunches. Because there are some assumptions about schooling and intellect and intelligent pursuits here, from that time or any time period, that are realistically way off. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1174792281873473474">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Like a maritime cutter, the narrative sails along smoothly, and much of the dialogue is in the distinctive argot of that windy Atlantic island: &amp;quot;When it\'s clear like ice and ribbed on the bottom--that\'s the killin\' frost. Your berries are dead. Good for moose and caribou pickin\'s. Now, there\'s them that picks \'em anyway, and that\'s why their jam is as tart as a whore\'s arse.&amp;quot;  With its partridgeberry patches, moose stew, and endless cups of tea, this is quintessential Newfoundland. After Lizzy dies, the nasty local pastor wants to put Kit in an orphanage and Josie in an appropriate institution. The compassionate Doctor Hodgins becomes their staunch defender against both do-gooders and those plotting Kit\'s downfall. This first novel is a female coming-of-age story of the rural variety, replete with endemic poverty, good-hearted and downright evil village people, and the constant irritant of Newfoundland\'s raw, nasty weather. But Saul wants peace, and he grudgingly comes to see that he’ll find it only through telling his story. With him, readers embark on a journey back through the life he’s led as a northern Ojibway, with all its joys and sorrows.\n\nWith compassion and insight, author Richard Wagamese traces through his fictional characters the decline of a culture and a cultural way. For Saul, taken forcibly from the land and his family when he’s sent to residential school, salvation comes for a while through his incredible gifts as a hockey player. But in the harsh realities of 1960s Canada, he battles obdurate racism and the spirit-destroying effects of cultural alienation and displacement.\n\nIndian Horse unfolds against the bleak loveliness of northern Ontario, all rock, marsh, bog and cedar. Wagamese writes with a spare beauty, penetrating the heart of a remarkable Ojibway man. Drawing on his great-grandfather’s mystical gift of vision, Saul Indian Horse comes to recognize the influence of everyday magic on his own life.
